        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to iam by: </span>
      my german teacher in 11th grade
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17377088-iam?shelf=transgender">transgender</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1801097660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9111177709571768857">First up: I have no idea where the subhead "romance of souls" comes from, because there is no romance in this book. Like, not even a hint at love story at any point, though the main character's unrequited crush/obsession is made a topic for a chapter, though it's really rather a device to make a plot turn than a plot point itself.<br/><br/>Now to the actual review: for the most part, I wanted to give this book 5 stars. I loved the beginning and much of the in between, one set of turns and twists in genera</span>
  <span id="freeText9111177709571768857" style="display:none">First up: I have no idea where the subhead "romance of souls" comes from, because there is no romance in this book. Like, not even a hint at love story at any point, though the main character's unrequited crush/obsession is made a topic for a chapter, though it's really rather a device to make a plot turn than a plot point itself.<br /><br />Now to the actual review: for the most part, I wanted to give this book 5 stars. I loved the beginning and much of the in between, one set of turns and twists in general <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <i> "The body is female."</i>  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> There would have been much potential to go into that deeper, and I am diappointed the author chose not to. <br />So I guess that's the first (if not main) reason why I didn't give that fifth star in the end.<br />Other reasons are that there are still many things that bothered me - parts were confusing and just simply didn't make sense, as if the author temporarily forgot to mention certain details.<br />The way of storytelling, especially time-wise, took some time getting used to as well. I was almost never confused or not sure at which point in time the current events were happening, and in that sense it was masterfully implemented, but I personally prefer a more chronological approach. The jumping between POVs and timelines felt too much like getting glimpses in the future just to go back and explain them, which ultimately left me feeling a little spoiled for the details to come. I was never bored or wanted to stop reading though.<br /><br />Then there's the ending, and in a way, the whole last part of the book. Previously, every new revelation was woven together with the beginning, but towards the end a whole new set of plot was opened which just didn't feel smooth compared to the rest, it felt removed and sort of pushed into the delicate net of storylines by brute force rather than natural connections that slowly build up.<br />It was not bad, just not particulary <i> good </i> and on par with the rest of the book.<br />Additionally, the epilogue itself felt rushed and extremely short compared to the other chapters, and answers some questions while bringing up whole new ones that didn't seem necessary to me.<br />That ending was what mostly made me not give five stars, to be honest, though it <i> did </i> make me look back on the title of the book with new feeling.<br /><br />Side note #1: the blurb confuses me. It consistently says "Andy" every time it should have said "Andrew".<br /><br />Side note #2: I read this book because several years ago, my German Teacher started talking about it randomly during a lesson. For at least an hour, us students asked her questions about the book because we were genuinely interested, and she was so enthusiastic about it that several of us ended up buying the book.</span>

<div id="review_106553230" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106553230" />
    <a title="M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13167-m"><img alt="M" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/106553230?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="M" href="/user/show/13167-m">M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer106553230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1809944200663146938">Five stars?? When's the last time THAT happened??<br/>Set This House in Order, a bizarre tale of not one but two people suffering from multiple personality disorder, takes you deep into the more frightening corners of the psyche, revealing an unlikely story of hope and renewal. <br/>An often misunderstood and Hollywood dramatized disorder, MPD is one of the more intricate and sophisticated coping mechanisms that a person can develop. What is most amazing about this tale in particular is how touching it is</span>
  <span id="freeText1809944200663146938" style="display:none">Five stars?? When's the last time THAT happened??<br />Set This House in Order, a bizarre tale of not one but two people suffering from multiple personality disorder, takes you deep into the more frightening corners of the psyche, revealing an unlikely story of hope and renewal. <br />An often misunderstood and Hollywood dramatized disorder, MPD is one of the more intricate and sophisticated coping mechanisms that a person can develop. What is most amazing about this tale in particular is how touching it is to see how much a person can be his own best friend - and worst enemy, at that, and sometimes simultaneously. We are hell bent for survival and protection at whatever cost, and this story reveals the layers that exist within every person, some of which are only pronounced through trauma. Let's just say, you think YOU'RE complicated?? Think again.<br />The story is narrated - mostly - by Andrew Gage, who does not have MPD as much as he is one of the personalities who is occupying a body. He has been assigned the task of controlling the body and the souls therein, in keeping with a method inspired by a rather innovative therapist, Dr. Gray, who insists that rather than integrate or expunge all the personalities, it is far more efficient to let them all coexist, but with a strict sense of order. The co narrator is Penny/Mouse, a severely traumatized woman who also suffers from MPD but doesn't know it.<br />The two meet and an adventure ensues as a lot of unresolved and long denied experiences begin to unravel, emphasizing Ruff's overall thesis - we need to deal with our stuff, no matter how tough (sorry). In fact, while the personalities are celebrated as allies, they are also withholding important information that, without ever confronting, will always hold a person back.<br />This one has it all - there is romance, mystery, suspense, horror and humor. It is fascinatingly original, thought provoking and gripping. The dialogue is real, the characters (all shmazillion of them) are well sketched, and I was always excited to pick it up again. For once a story was not too long.<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="1809944200663146938" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1373691278" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1373691278" />
    <a title="Alicia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/868816-alicia"><img alt="Alicia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443678787p2/868816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1373691278?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alicia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alicia" href="/user/show/868816-alicia">Alicia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/868816-alicia?shelf=2015-reads">2015-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1373691278" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11197284112671530791">I had never heard of this book, but based on the rating and reviews, and ultimately how interesting it sounded I wanted to give it a try. And when I opened to the first page my immediate thought was... oh crap, this is going to get too confusing, I don't know if I could do this. But soon enough, it all started to come together, and I had to keep reading. <br/><br/>I won't go into the plot, but Andrew Gage, the main character, is so completely likable, I adored him. Only Andrew was actually only recently b</span>
  <span id="freeText11197284112671530791" style="display:none">I had never heard of this book, but based on the rating and reviews, and ultimately how interesting it sounded I wanted to give it a try. And when I opened to the first page my immediate thought was... oh crap, this is going to get too confusing, I don't know if I could do this. But soon enough, it all started to come together, and I had to keep reading. <br /><br />I won't go into the plot, but Andrew Gage, the main character, is so completely likable, I adored him. Only Andrew was actually only recently born, a newer personality in charge of Andy's body. Just as likable was his new co-worker Penny/Mouse, another person who has MPD, only unlike Andrew, she is not yet aware of this, she only knows that she keeps losing time and finds herself in less than ideal situations. <br /><br />The book could have gone two ways; it could have been extremely heavy and depressing, given Andy and Penny's childhood, or, it could have gone onto the overly quirky side. But I think the author managed to avoid either and there was a good balance of lightness while not undermining their trauma. And thankfully, despite what the title might suggest, there is never really a true "romance" between the two of them, which made it all the better. <br /><br />With that said, I will warn that for the first half of the book, Andy and Penny's backstories are insinuated, but later on, especially with Andy, it is faced head-on, so readers beware if you are sensitive to themes of abuse done onto a child.<br /><br />Okay. So there was a twist I was kind of expecting, but didn't happen to my relief. And then there was a twist that did happen, that I was <i>not</i> expecting at all lol. The book also lost some steam near the end, but overall, I thought this was a very interesting story, featuring a topic I don't know too much about, with very enjoyable characters and though I don't do this very often at all, there might be a reread in the future. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11197284112671530791" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_44921912" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44921912" />
    <a title="Eric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1390607-eric"><img alt="Eric" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44921912?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric" href="/user/show/1390607-eric">Eric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44921912" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17226772513471019912">This is another book club book, and Tiptree award winner.<br/><br/>I really enjoyed this book - the narrative voice especially drove it for me. Within the first three pages, I pinged something about the main character that turned out to be a big reveal later in the book, but I didn't feel ripped off - there was just something about the protagonist that made it completely unsurprising.<br/><br/>The story is basically about a person with multiple personalities, who manages them by having an internal "house" where the</span>
  <span id="freeText17226772513471019912" style="display:none">This is another book club book, and Tiptree award winner.<br /><br />I really enjoyed this book - the narrative voice especially drove it for me. Within the first three pages, I pinged something about the main character that turned out to be a big reveal later in the book, but I didn't feel ripped off - there was just something about the protagonist that made it completely unsurprising.<br /><br />The story is basically about a person with multiple personalities, who manages them by having an internal "house" where they all live. The main personality can call different personalities forth to actually control the body for a while, or have them stand in the house's "pulpit", where they can watch events in the physical world and advise him. It presents an interesting idea of how someone with multiple personalities can build a life that will function with the rest of society, without having to reintegrate all of the personalities.<br /><br />While that is interesting in and of itself, the story goes on to where his boss hires another multiple, expecting him to help her. The problem is that she is living with untreated DID, and her primary personality really doesn't even realize it.<br /><br />The handling of two characters with several unique facets requires a lot of work on the part of an author, and Ruff handles it deftly. There is a major part of the story where he builds a strange meta-tension, where I found myself wondering if he was going to take the story a certain place, but he didn't. It was interesting to have those moments of stepping back and wondering how much the author was toying with me, and I liked it.<br /><br />My only real beef is that the ending folded together a bit too pat. It came to a nice climax, but the denouement didn't really satisfy. It's a small quibble, though, for a book that's solid through and through. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17226772513471019912" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/527660675?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt De" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt De" href="/user/show/17184456-matt-de">Matt De</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer527660675" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18260422982877483770">I liked this book a lot. If you read it in close proximity to some of his other works it will be obvious that either the author did too, and took extra special care in crafting it, or his editor did and kept on sending it back until it they thought it really was the best the author could do. The quality of the prose is significantly smoother and more refined than his other works, in my opinion. <br/><br/>His previous two books suffer from a "cast of thousands" problem. Lots of characters that are hard to </span>
  <span id="freeText18260422982877483770" style="display:none">I liked this book a lot. If you read it in close proximity to some of his other works it will be obvious that either the author did too, and took extra special care in crafting it, or his editor did and kept on sending it back until it they thought it really was the best the author could do. The quality of the prose is significantly smoother and more refined than his other works, in my opinion. <br /><br />His previous two books suffer from a "cast of thousands" problem. Lots of characters that are hard to keep track of and can only be developed so much if you are going to fit them all in. This one sort of reigns that in without really actually getting rid of the very large cast. <br /><br />That being said, the backdrop for a chunk of the story is a late 80s/early 90s VR tech startup. I'm of the right age that I can fill that in with a pretty detailed imagining informed by being on both the consumer side and producer side of such things when they were going on. I can picture that coming off as some poorly imagined and thought out approximation of a tech start up to someone significantly younger. If it breaks that way for you, I can see that being a distracting annoyance. If you were born in the 90s or later, be prepared to ignore the set dressing and focus more on the interpersonal dynamic for that portion of the book. <br /><br />It has it's happy bits and sad bits and charming bits, and generally gets you attached to a handful of characters enough that knowing it can't end up well for everyone seems like a tragedy, even if a bit of a prosaic one. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18260422982877483770" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41607836?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41607836" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9775789104368983080">As many of my reviews say, I'm quite interested in brain (dis)orders. This is a novel about multiple personality disorder (MPD). I was attracted to it, and yet overwhelmed at the same time -- thinking it might be a tad complicated (not sure why exactly) but while it certainly is potentially complicated to keep track of one body with many souls and personalities the author does a great job of keeping the story straight for the reader. In this case the main character is a MPD that has "set it's ho</span>
  <span id="freeText9775789104368983080" style="display:none">As many of my reviews say, I'm quite interested in brain (dis)orders. This is a novel about multiple personality disorder (MPD). I was attracted to it, and yet overwhelmed at the same time -- thinking it might be a tad complicated (not sure why exactly) but while it certainly is potentially complicated to keep track of one body with many souls and personalities the author does a great job of keeping the story straight for the reader. In this case the main character is a MPD that has "set it's house in order" and various personalities are kept in check and the soul in charge of the body keeps them functioning in the world. However, when Andy meets another MPD and is in fact asked to help her find order for her "society" the structure of both their "houses" and "societies" (their mental state) is challenged and put at risk. Much of it involved confronting massive trauma from their childhood, instilled by crazy abusive parents. The two characters work together and through this we learn quite a bit about MPD, about how hard it is to cope and also how one can, and as a novel it was totally engaging and throughly enjoyable!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9775789104368983080" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/7324264-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060195625" href="/book/show/797948.Set_This_House_in_Order">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7324264-john?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7324264-john?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer262073384" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10633511369171618191">"Set This House In Order" is Matt Ruff's finest work of fiction to date, brilliantly adding to a splendid body of work that includes such classics as his literary debut "Fool On The Hill" and the Ayn Rand-influenced cyberpunk novel "Sewer, Gas, Electric: The Public Works Trilogy". He offers a fascinating twist on the coming-of-age tale, exploring the lives of the multiple personalities inhabiting the bodies of Andrew Gage and Penny Driver. Like Jonathan Lethem in "Motherless Brooklyn", Ruff writ</span>
  <span id="freeText10633511369171618191" style="display:none">"Set This House In Order" is Matt Ruff's finest work of fiction to date, brilliantly adding to a splendid body of work that includes such classics as his literary debut "Fool On The Hill" and the Ayn Rand-influenced cyberpunk novel "Sewer, Gas, Electric: The Public Works Trilogy". He offers a fascinating twist on the coming-of-age tale, exploring the lives of the multiple personalities inhabiting the bodies of Andrew Gage and Penny Driver. Like Jonathan Lethem in "Motherless Brooklyn", Ruff writes eloquently and with much compassion about two characters afflicted with a severe personality disorder. None of his splendid prose lapses into cliche or melodramatic writing. It's one of the few books I have read lately that I found almost impossible to put down, compelled to read vast portions of the novel at one clip. Without a doubt, Matt Ruff has become the most distinguished writer ever to have graduated from New York City's prestigious Stuyvesant High School. He is also among my generation's most talented writers, comparable in quality to the likes of Jonathan Lethem, Jeffrey Eugenides and Michael Chabon. <br /><br />(Reposted from my 2003 Amazon review)</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15872508464953463012">I have to be honoest and say that this book deals with multiple personality disorders and it is beautifully written. It is realistically written and the pain that the two main characters have to go through, the traumatic events that lead to the splitting of their souls is so wrenching and affecting that I couldn't finish the book. I just couldn't take it. But I will remember this book for a long time. It was recommended by a very good friend (an AP English teacher) who usually reads chick lit an</span>
  <span id="freeText15872508464953463012" style="display:none">I have to be honoest and say that this book deals with multiple personality disorders and it is beautifully written. It is realistically written and the pain that the two main characters have to go through, the traumatic events that lead to the splitting of their souls is so wrenching and affecting that I couldn't finish the book. I just couldn't take it. But I will remember this book for a long time. It was recommended by a very good friend (an AP English teacher) who usually reads chick lit and has a very different genre that she gravitates towards. I had recommended Bad Monkeys (another of Ruff's books) and she enjoyed it. Her husband, who does read and enjoy the same books as I do (urband fantasy, sci-fi), loved this book as well. She was sure if she and her husband both loved it, that I would as well. <br /><br />I do think it is a gorgeous book. But I have gotten to that stage, where I can't go through some painful stories. If you can handle it though, I highly recommend giving this book a try.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1516830394" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2416707960765893022">I don't know anything about Multiple Personality Disorder (Dissociative Identity Disorder), but I am prepared to believe it could be like this. The author presents a very compelling and consistent view of what it might be like. He has two main characters: one of them has his MPD under control and the other is completely out of control. Perhaps a bit predictably, the story develops by one of them gradually losing control and the other gaining control. And they work together to bring the story to </span>
  <span id="freeText2416707960765893022" style="display:none">I don't know anything about Multiple Personality Disorder (Dissociative Identity Disorder), but I am prepared to believe it could be like this. The author presents a very compelling and consistent view of what it might be like. He has two main characters: one of them has his MPD under control and the other is completely out of control. Perhaps a bit predictably, the story develops by one of them gradually losing control and the other gaining control. And they work together to bring the story to a resolution.<br /><br />I did enjoy reading this, and it is probably more like 3.5 stars, but I couldn't quite bring myself to up it to 4. It is a very well put together story with a believable interior world in which the character live, and that's no mean feat when dealing with MPD. But, in the end, I was just a bit disappointed that it played out in a rather predictable fashion.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JTPG6zn8+M01xHAb9m2JeZGePNiKEvllUuoVd0RDHsSm9g1tOm5hc2CwVe6JybliwMa6BKpA0svtGpjKg3Nogw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('D5uTeoDdTCLXs9RueSVve0v9Wi1cgHpRv+pG7cofqJeMXlj8g0/VnILH8ZsGgV9gGqXc8XzSUf8AGstQDS/e0A==')})
  }

He is painfully unaware that these torments are due to the secret manipulations of New Renaissance, an experimental organization that is testing the age-old idea that art results from suffering. Since culture is so significantly influenced by music, movies, and television, New Renaissance hopes to improve the mindless mainstream by raising writers who emphasize artistic quality over commerce. As part of its top-secret sub-project, New Renaissance hires reluctant ex-musician Harlan Eiffler to manipulate its most promising prodigy, Vincent. Wickedly antisocial and deeply disgusted by what passes for entertainment in the twenty-first century, Harlan clandestinely pulls the strings so that Vincent remains a true artist. Suite au décès de la grand-mère, les seules «survivantes» d’une secte qui a beaucoup fait parler d’elle à Rivière-aux-Hiboux et dont la plup<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText234035143065167362\" style=\"display:none\">Une jeune femme née dans d’étranges circonstances doit se rendre avec sa mère sur les lieux de sa conception. Suite au décès de la grand-mère, les seules «survivantes» d’une secte qui a beaucoup fait parler d’elle à Rivière-aux-Hiboux et dont la plupart des membres sont morts plusieurs années auparavant dans un suicide collectif sont les héritiers de la maison où tout a commencé. En ces bois profonds est une descente infernale dans les méandres de la folie, de laquelle cette jeune narratrice cherchera à tout prix à trouver le sens. Ainsi se dévoileront les détails d’une histoire familiale où les croyances l’ont emporté sur la raison, ponctuée de la légende autochtone du lac Misiginebig et des aspirations messianique d’un certain Nicolas Jones, guérisseur patenté et gourou. Investie d’une quête, l’adolescente ne pourra que plonger elle aussi dans cette folie qui, après tout, coule dans son sang… François Lévesque poursuit la même recherche stylistique expérimentée dans En attendant Russell (jeux sur la répétition, phrases courtes et scindées, aux limites de la poésie), mais dans un récit des plus noirs, un thriller rural aux atmosphères inquiétantes et aux retournements sinistres, et qui se veut également une forme d’hommage à celles qu’il nomme ses «marraines gothiques»; Marie-Claire Blais et Anne Hébert. L’auteur prouve une fois de plus sa grande maîtrise de l’écriture des ambiances et de l’environnement dans un récit bien ficelé qui trace les minces lignes de la limite entre lucidité et Pas étonnant, donc, que son premier roman, Soutien-gorge rose et veston noir, soit fortement inspiré du climat urbain branché, pétillant et osé qui a fait le succès de cette émission.  L\'histoire est découpée en épisodes qui relatent une année dans la vie tumultueuse de Chloé Saint-Mars. Recherchiste pigiste, la jeune femme de 28 ans est surtout irrémédiablement célibataire, ayant relégué aux oubliettes l\'amour des contes de fées auquel elle croyait à 10 ans. Avec Juliette et Antoine, ses inséparables alter ego, elle a rédigé le Manifeste du célibat qui clame :la vie plate des gens casés, c\'est pas pour nous autres. Bien sûr, l\'amour pointera son nez, et Chloé devra réviser ses positions. En dépit de longueurs et d\'inutiles répétitions, ce roman est le portrait réussi d\'une jeunesse privilégiée et bourrée de contradictions. Les filles ne croient pas à l\'amour, mais passent leur temps à en parler. Ça baise beaucoup, ça boit encore plus, ça parle cru, mais ça rêve de mariage en blanc, de bébés et d\'amour éternel, un amour encore vif, patiné et enrichi. Rafaële Germain, qui a aussi 28 ans et dont les chroniques dans La Presse lui ont déjà acquis un grand nombre de lecteurs, prend le pouls de sa génération avec empathie, humour et une bonne dose de Dave est sur le point de réaliser que oui. Ayant réussi à s\'évader en compagnie de trois autres détenus – dont un violeur particulièrement fêlé –, il se réfugie dans la maison isolée d\'une psychiatre attachée au pénitencier, l\'énigmatique Vivianne Léveillé. Pendant cette nuit d\'épouvante, ils iront de surprise en surprise… comme le lecteur. «En moins de deux minutes, ils sont passés de la science-fiction glauque au burlesque surréaliste. Mais dans quelle maison de fous sont-ils tombés ?»  Il ne faut pas trop en révéler afin de ne pas gâcher le plaisir (horrifique, bien sûr) que distille Oniria. Patrick Senécal s\'amuse visiblement dans ce délirant jeu de massacres, où il place des personnages typés mais bien définis dans l\'inconfortable position de rats de laboratoire. Très bien construite, l\'intrigue de ce thriller fantastique happe le lecteur dans un suspense aussi cocasse que cauchemardesque. Peuplé de créatures extravagantes, ce roman fantaisiste du très populaire auteur est sans doute plus anodin que son précédent, Les Sept Jours du Talion, mais il est diablement divertissant. --Marie       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/40577.Matt_Ruff">About Matt Ruff</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/40577.Matt_Ruff"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1597587995p3/40577.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/40577.Matt_Ruff">Matt Ruff

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4919419711263762810">I was born in New York City in 1965. I decided I wanted to be a fiction writer when I was five years old and spent my childhood and adolescence learning how to tell stories. At Cornell University I wrote what would become my first published novel, Fool on the Hill, as my senior thesis in Honors English. My professor Alison Lurie helped me find an agent, and within six months of my college graduati</span>
  <span id="freeText4919419711263762810" style="display:none">I was born in New York City in 1965. I decided I wanted to be a fiction writer when I was five years old and spent my childhood and adolescence learning how to tell stories. At Cornell University I wrote what would become my first published novel, Fool on the Hill, as my senior thesis in Honors English. My professor Alison Lurie helped me find an agent, and within six months of my college graduation Fool on the Hill had been sold to Atlantic Monthly Press. Through a combination of timely foreign rights sales, the generous support of family and friends, occasional grant money, and a slowly accumulating back list, I’ve managed to make novel-writing my primary occupation ever since.<br /><br />My third novel, Set This House in Order, marked a critical turning point in my career after it won the James Tiptree, Jr. Award, a Washington State Book Award, and a Pacific Northwest Booksellers Association Award, and helped me secure a fellowship from the National Endowment for the Arts. My fourth novel, Bad Monkeys, also won multiple awards and is being developed as a film, with Margot Robbie attached to star. My sixth novel, Lovecraft Country, has been produced as an HBO series by Misha Green, Jordan Peele, and J.J. Abrams. It will debut on Sunday, August 16.<br /><br />In 1998 I married my best friend, the researcher and rare-book expert Lisa Gold. We live in Seattle, Washington.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4919419711263762810" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
